    window.loadScript = (url, { charset = 'UTF-8', ...attributes } = {}, location = 'head') => {
      const existingScript = document.querySelector('script[src="' + url + '"]');
      if (existingScript) {
        return;
      }

      const script = document.createElement('script');
      script.src = url;
      script.type = 'text/javascript';

      script.async = true;
      script.charset = charset;

      if (attributes) {
        Object.entries(attributes).forEach(([key, value]) => {
          script.setAttribute(key, value);
        });
      }

      if (location === 'head') {
        document.head.appendChild(script);
      } else {
        document.body.appendChild(script);
      }

      return script;
    };

uid="1J3h5J" component-url="/_astro/GoogleAnalytics.D0enFA2_.js" component-export="GoogleAnalytics" renderer-url="/_astro/client.BMDBmNz6.js" props="{&quot;title&quot;:[0,&quot;Fixing request smuggling vulnerabilities in Pingora OSS deployments&quot;],&quot;canonical&quot;:[0,&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/pingora-oss-smuggling-vulnerabilities&quot;],&quot;info&quot;:[0,{&quot;id&quot;:[0,&quot;1b0iJgL57wbfiLHXhEjuwR&quot;],&quot;title&quot;:[0,&quot;Fixing request smuggling vulnerabilities in Pingora OSS deployments&quot;],&quot;slug&quot;:[0,&quot;pingora-oss-smuggling-vulnerabilities&quot;],&quot;excerpt&quot;:[0,&quot;Today weâre disclosing request smuggling vulnerabilities when our open source Pingora service is deployed as an ingress proxy and how weâve fixed them in Pingora 0.8.0. &quot;],&quot;featured&quot;:[0,false],&quot;html&quot;:[0,&quot;&lt;p&gt;In December 2025, Cloudflare received reports of HTTP/1.x request smuggling vulnerabilities in the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Pingora open source&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; framework when Pingora is used to build an ingress proxy. Today we are discussing how these vulnerabilities work and how we patched them in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/releases/tag/0.8.0\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Pingora 0.8.0&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The vulnerabilities are &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cve.org/CVERecord?id=CVE-2026-2833\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CVE-2026-2833&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cve.org/CVERecord?id=CVE-2026-2835\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CVE-2026-2835&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cve.org/CVERecord?id=CVE-2026-2836\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CVE-2026-2836&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. These issues were responsibly reported to us by Rajat Raghav (xclow3n) through our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/disclosure/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Bug Bounty Program&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Cloudflareâs CDN and customer traffic were not affected&lt;/b&gt;, our investigation found. &lt;b&gt;No action is needed for Cloudflare customers, and no impact was detected.&lt;/b&gt;Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Due to the architecture of Cloudflareâs network, these vulnerabilities could not be exploited: Pingora is not used as an ingress proxy in Cloudflareâs CDN.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, these issues impact standalone Pingora deployments exposed to the Internet, and may enable an attacker to:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Bypass Pingora proxy-layer security controls&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Desync HTTP request/responses with backends for cross-user hijacking attacks (session or credential theft)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Poison Pingora proxy-layer caches retrieving content from shared backends&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;We have released &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/releases/tag/0.8.0\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Pingora 0.8.0&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; with fixes and hardening. While Cloudflare customers were not affected, we strongly recommend users of the Pingora framework to &lt;b&gt;upgrade as soon as possible.&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;what-was-the-vulnerability\&quot;&gt;What was the vulnerability?&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#what-was-the-vulnerability\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The reports described a few different HTTP/1 attack payloads that could cause desync attacks. Such requests could cause the proxy and backend to disagree about where the request body ends, allowing a second request to be âsmuggledâ past proxyâlayer checks. The researcher provided a proof-of-concept to validate how a basic Pingora reverse proxy misinterpreted request body lengths and forwarded those requests to server backends such as Node/Express or uvicorn.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Upon receiving the reports, our engineering team immediately investigated and validated that, as the reporter also confirmed, the Cloudflare CDN itself was not vulnerable. However, the team did also validate that vulnerabilities exist when Pingora acts as the ingress proxy to shared backends.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;By design, the Pingora framework &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/how-we-built-pingora-the-proxy-that-connects-cloudflare-to-the-internet/#design-decisions\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;does allow&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; edge case HTTP requests or responses that are not strictly RFC compliant, because we must accept this sort of traffic for customers with legacy HTTP stacks. But this leniency has limits to avoid exposing Cloudflare itself to vulnerabilities.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In this case, Pingora had non-RFC-compliant interpretations of request bodies within its HTTP/1 stack that allowed these desync attacks to exist. Pingora deployments within Cloudflare are not directly exposed to ingress traffic, and we found that production traffic that arrived at Pingora services were not subject to these misinterpretations. Thus, the attacks were not exploitable on Cloudflare traffic itself, unlike a &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/resolving-a-request-smuggling-vulnerability-in-pingora/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;previous Pingora smuggling vulnerability&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; disclosed in May 2025.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weâll explain, case-by-case, how these attack payloads worked.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h3 id=\&quot;1-premature-upgrade-without-101-handshake\&quot;&gt;1. Premature upgrade without 101 handshake&lt;/h3&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#1-premature-upgrade-without-101-handshake\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The first report showed that a request with an &lt;code&gt;Upgrade&lt;/code&gt; header value would cause Pingora to pass through subsequent bytes on the HTTP connection immediately, before the backend had accepted an upgrade (by returning &lt;code&gt;101 Switching Protocols&lt;/code&gt;). The attacker could thus pipeline a second HTTP request after the upgrade request on the same connection:&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;GET / HTTP/1.1\nHost: example.com\nUpgrade: foo\n\n\nGET /admin HTTP/1.1\nHost: example.com&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;Pingora would parse only the initial request, then treat the remaining buffered bytes as the âupgradedâ stream and forward them directly to the backend in a âpassthroughâ mode &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/server.rs#L797\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;due to the Upgrade header&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (until the response &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/server.rs#L523\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;was received&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is not at all how the HTTP/1.1 Upgrade process per &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9110#field.upgrade\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;RFC 9110&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; is intended to work. The subsequent bytes should &lt;i&gt;only&lt;/i&gt; be interpreted as part of an upgraded stream if a &lt;code&gt;101 Switching Protocols&lt;/code&gt; header is received, and if a &lt;code&gt;200 OK&lt;/code&gt; response is received instead, the subsequent bytes should continue to be interpreted as HTTP.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/2IYHyGkABpNA0e09wiiGpY/4f51ea330c2d266260f6361dd9d64d79/image4.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1755\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;An attacker that sends an Upgrade request, then pipelines a partial HTTP request may cause a desync attack. Pingora will incorrectly interpret both as the same upgraded request, even if the backend server declines the upgrade with a 200.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Via the improper pass-through, a Pingora deployment that received a non-101 response could still forward the second partial HTTP request to the upstream as-is, bypassing any Pingora userâdefined ACL-handling or WAF logic, and poison the connection to the upstream so that a subsequent request from a different user could improperly receive the &lt;code&gt;/admin&lt;/code&gt; response.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/oIwatu6gaMoJHCCs95sFN/8ea94ee8f04be6f7f00474168b382180/image3.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1755\&quot; height=\&quot;648\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;After the attack payload, Pingora and the backend server are now âdesynced.â The backend server will wait until it thinks the rest of the partial /attack request header that Pingora forwarded is complete. When Pingora forwards a different userâs request, the two headers are combined from the backend serverâs perspective, and the attacker has now poisoned the other userâs response.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weâve since &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/824bdeefc61e121cc8861de1b35e8e8f39026ecd\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;patched&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; Pingora to switch the interpretation of subsequent bytes only once the upstream responds with &lt;code&gt;101 Switching Protocols&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We verified Cloudflare was &lt;b&gt;not affected&lt;/b&gt; for two reasons:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;The ingress CDN proxies do not have this improper behavior.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;The clients to our internal Pingora services do not attempt to &lt;a href=\&quot;https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTTP_pipelining\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;pipeline&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; HTTP/1 requests. Furthermore, the Pingora service these clients talk directly with disables keep-alive on these &lt;code&gt;Upgrade&lt;/code&gt; requests by injecting a &lt;code&gt;Connection: close&lt;/code&gt; header; this prevents additional requests that would be sent â and subsequently smuggled â over the same connection.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h3 id=\&quot;2-http-1-0-close-delimiting-and-transfer-encoding\&quot;&gt;2. HTTP/1.0, close-delimiting, and transfer-encoding&lt;/h3&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#2-http-1-0-close-delimiting-and-transfer-encoding\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The reporter also demonstrated what &lt;i&gt;appeared&lt;/i&gt; to be a more classic âCL.TEâ desync-type attack, where the Pingora proxy would use Content-Length as framing while the backend would use Transfer-Encoding as framing:&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;GET / HTTP/1.0\nHost: example.com\nConnection: keep-alive\nTransfer-Encoding: identity, chunked\nContent-Length: 29\n\n0\n\nGET /admin HTTP/1.1\nX:\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;In the reporterâs example, Pingora would treat all subsequent bytes after the first GET / request header as part of that requestâs body, but the node.js backend server would interpret the body as chunked and ending at the zero-length chunk. There are actually a few things going on here:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pingoraâs chunked encoding recognition was quite barebones (only checking for whether &lt;code&gt;Transfer-Encoding&lt;/code&gt; was â&lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/9ac75d0356f449d26097e08bf49af14de6271727/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/common.rs#L146\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;chunked&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;â) and assumed that there could only be one encoding or &lt;code&gt;Transfer-Encoding&lt;/code&gt; header. But the RFC only &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-2.4.1\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;mandates&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; that the &lt;i&gt;final&lt;/i&gt; encoding must be &lt;code&gt;chunked&lt;/code&gt; to apply chunked framing. So per RFC, this request should have a chunked message body (if it were not HTTP/1.0 â more on that below).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pingora was &lt;i&gt;also &lt;/i&gt;not actually using the &lt;code&gt;Content-Length&lt;/code&gt; (because the Transfer-Encoding overrode the Content-Length &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-2.3\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;per RFC&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;). Because of the unrecognized Transfer-Encoding and the HTTP/1.0 version, the request body was &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/server.rs#L817\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;instead treated as close-delimited&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (which means that the response bodyâs end is marked by closure of the underlying transport connection). An absence of framing headers would also trigger the same misinterpretation on HTTP/1.0. Although response bodies are allowed to be close-delimited, request bodies are &lt;i&gt;never&lt;/i&gt; close-delimited. In fact, this clarification is now explicitly called out as a separate note in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-4.1\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;RFC 9112&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is an HTTP/1.0 request that &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#appendix-C.2.3-1\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;did not define&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; Transfer-Encoding. The RFC &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.1-16\&quot;&gt;mandates&lt;/a&gt; that HTTP/1.0 requests containing Transfer-Encoding must âtreat the message as if the framing is faultyâ and close the connection. Parsers such as the ones in nginx and hyper just reject these requests to avoid ambiguous framing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/1jLbMNafmF96toxAPxj2Cm/8561b96a56dc0fc654476e33d0f34888/image2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1755\&quot; height=\&quot;1082\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;When an attacker pipelines a partial HTTP request header after the HTTP/1.0 + Transfer-Encoding request, Pingora would incorrectly interpret that partial header as part of the same request, rather than as a distinct request. This enables the same kind of desync attack as described in the premature Upgrade example.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This spoke to a more fundamental misreading of the RFC particularly in terms of response vs. request message framing. Weâve since fixed the improper &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/7f7166d62fa916b9f11b2eb8f9e3c4999e8b9023\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;multiple Transfer-Encoding parsing&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, adhere strictly to the request length guidelines such that HTTP request bodies can &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/40c3c1e9a43a86b38adeab8da7a2f6eba68b83ad\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;never be considered close-delimited&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and reject &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/fc904c0d2c679be522de84729ec73f0bd344963d\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;invalid Content-Length&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/87e2e2fb37edf9be33e3b1d04726293ae6bf2052\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;HTTP/1.0 + Transfer-Encoding&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; request messages. Further protections weâve added include &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/d3d2cf5ef4eca1e5d327fe282ec4b4ee474350c6\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;rejecting&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9110#name-connect\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CONNECT&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; requests by default because the HTTP proxy logic doesnât currently treat CONNECT as special for the purposes of CONNECT upgrade proxying, and these requests have special &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-2.2\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;message framing rules&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. (Note that incoming CONNECT requests are &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/fundamentals/concepts/traffic-flow-cloudflare/#cloudflares-network\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;rejected&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; by the Cloudflare CDN.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When we investigated and instrumented our services internally, we found no requests arriving at our Pingora services that would have been misinterpreted. We found that downstream proxy layers in the CDN would forward as HTTP/1.1 only, reject ambiguous framing such as invalid Content-Length, and only forward a single &lt;code&gt;Transfer-Encoding: chunked&lt;/code&gt; header for chunked requests.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h3 id=\&quot;3-cache-key-construction\&quot;&gt;3. Cache key construction&lt;/h3&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#3-cache-key-construction\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The researcher also reported one other cache poisoning vulnerability regarding default &lt;code&gt;CacheKey&lt;/code&gt; construction. The &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-cache/src/key.rs#L218\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;naive default implementation&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; factored in only the URI path (without other factors such as host header or upstream server HTTP scheme), which meant different hosts using the same HTTP path could collide and poison each otherâs cache.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This would affect users of the alpha proxy caching feature who chose to use the default &lt;code&gt;CacheKey&lt;/code&gt; implementation. We have since &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/257b59ada28ed6cac039f67d0b71f414efa0ab6e\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;removed that default&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, because while using something like HTTP scheme + host + URI makes sense for many applications, we want users to be careful when constructing their cache keys for themselves. If their proxy logic will conditionally adjust the URI or method on the upstream request, for example, that logic likely also must be factored into the cache key scheme to avoid poisoning.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Internally, Cloudflareâs &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/cache/how-to/cache-keys/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;default cache key&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; uses a number of factors to prevent cache key poisoning, and never made use of the previously provided default.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;recommendation\&quot;&gt;Recommendation&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#recommendation\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;If you use Pingora as a proxy, upgrade to &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/releases/tag/0.8.0\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Pingora 0.8.0&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; at your earliest convenience.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We apologize for the impact this vulnerability may have had on Pingora users. As Pingora earns its place as critical Internet infrastructure beyond Cloudflare, we believe itâs important for the framework to promote use of strict RFC compliance by default and will continue this effort. Very few users of the framework should have to deal with the same âwild Internetâ that Cloudflare does. Our intention is that stricter adherence to the latest RFC standards by default will harden security for Pingora users and move the Internet as a whole toward best practices.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;disclosure-and-response-timeline\&quot;&gt;Disclosure and response timeline&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#disclosure-and-response-timeline\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;- 2025â12â02: Upgradeâbased smuggling reported via bug bounty.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026â01â13: TransferâEncoding / HTTP/1.0 parsing issues reported.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026-01-18: Default cache key construction issue reported.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026â01â29 to 2026â02â13: Fixes validated with the reporter. Today we are discussing how these vulnerabilities work and how we patched them in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/releases/tag/0.8.0\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Pingora 0.8.0&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The vulnerabilities are &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cve.org/CVERecord?id=CVE-2026-2833\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CVE-2026-2833&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cve.org/CVERecord?id=CVE-2026-2835\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CVE-2026-2835&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cve.org/CVERecord?id=CVE-2026-2836\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CVE-2026-2836&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. These issues were responsibly reported to us by Rajat Raghav (xclow3n) through our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/disclosure/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Bug Bounty Program&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Cloudflareâs CDN and customer traffic were not affected&lt;/b&gt;, our investigation found. &lt;b&gt;No action is needed for Cloudflare customers, and no impact was detected.&lt;/b&gt;Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Due to the architecture of Cloudflareâs network, these vulnerabilities could not be exploited: Pingora is not used as an ingress proxy in Cloudflareâs CDN.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, these issues impact standalone Pingora deployments exposed to the Internet, and may enable an attacker to:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Bypass Pingora proxy-layer security controls&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Desync HTTP request/responses with backends for cross-user hijacking attacks (session or credential theft)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Poison Pingora proxy-layer caches retrieving content from shared backends&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;We have released &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/releases/tag/0.8.0\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Pingora 0.8.0&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; with fixes and hardening. While Cloudflare customers were not affected, we strongly recommend users of the Pingora framework to &lt;b&gt;upgrade as soon as possible.&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;what-was-the-vulnerability\&quot;&gt;What was the vulnerability?&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#what-was-the-vulnerability\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The reports described a few different HTTP/1 attack payloads that could cause desync attacks. Such requests could cause the proxy and backend to disagree about where the request body ends, allowing a second request to be âsmuggledâ past proxyâlayer checks. The researcher provided a proof-of-concept to validate how a basic Pingora reverse proxy misinterpreted request body lengths and forwarded those requests to server backends such as Node/Express or uvicorn.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Upon receiving the reports, our engineering team immediately investigated and validated that, as the reporter also confirmed, the Cloudflare CDN itself was not vulnerable. However, the team did also validate that vulnerabilities exist when Pingora acts as the ingress proxy to shared backends.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;By design, the Pingora framework &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/how-we-built-pingora-the-proxy-that-connects-cloudflare-to-the-internet/#design-decisions\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;does allow&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; edge case HTTP requests or responses that are not strictly RFC compliant, because we must accept this sort of traffic for customers with legacy HTTP stacks. But this leniency has limits to avoid exposing Cloudflare itself to vulnerabilities.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In this case, Pingora had non-RFC-compliant interpretations of request bodies within its HTTP/1 stack that allowed these desync attacks to exist. Pingora deployments within Cloudflare are not directly exposed to ingress traffic, and we found that production traffic that arrived at Pingora services were not subject to these misinterpretations. Thus, the attacks were not exploitable on Cloudflare traffic itself, unlike a &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/resolving-a-request-smuggling-vulnerability-in-pingora/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;previous Pingora smuggling vulnerability&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; disclosed in May 2025.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weâll explain, case-by-case, how these attack payloads worked.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h3 id=\&quot;1-premature-upgrade-without-101-handshake\&quot;&gt;1. Premature upgrade without 101 handshake&lt;/h3&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#1-premature-upgrade-without-101-handshake\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The first report showed that a request with an &lt;code&gt;Upgrade&lt;/code&gt; header value would cause Pingora to pass through subsequent bytes on the HTTP connection immediately, before the backend had accepted an upgrade (by returning &lt;code&gt;101 Switching Protocols&lt;/code&gt;). The attacker could thus pipeline a second HTTP request after the upgrade request on the same connection:&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;GET / HTTP/1.1\nHost: example.com\nUpgrade: foo\n\n\nGET /admin HTTP/1.1\nHost: example.com&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;Pingora would parse only the initial request, then treat the remaining buffered bytes as the âupgradedâ stream and forward them directly to the backend in a âpassthroughâ mode &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/server.rs#L797\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;due to the Upgrade header&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (until the response &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/server.rs#L523\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;was received&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is not at all how the HTTP/1.1 Upgrade process per &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9110#field.upgrade\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;RFC 9110&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; is intended to work. The subsequent bytes should &lt;i&gt;only&lt;/i&gt; be interpreted as part of an upgraded stream if a &lt;code&gt;101 Switching Protocols&lt;/code&gt; header is received, and if a &lt;code&gt;200 OK&lt;/code&gt; response is received instead, the subsequent bytes should continue to be interpreted as HTTP.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/2IYHyGkABpNA0e09wiiGpY/4f51ea330c2d266260f6361dd9d64d79/image4.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1755\&quot; height=\&quot;1125\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;An attacker that sends an Upgrade request, then pipelines a partial HTTP request may cause a desync attack. Pingora will incorrectly interpret both as the same upgraded request, even if the backend server declines the upgrade with a 200.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Via the improper pass-through, a Pingora deployment that received a non-101 response could still forward the second partial HTTP request to the upstream as-is, bypassing any Pingora userâdefined ACL-handling or WAF logic, and poison the connection to the upstream so that a subsequent request from a different user could improperly receive the &lt;code&gt;/admin&lt;/code&gt; response.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/oIwatu6gaMoJHCCs95sFN/8ea94ee8f04be6f7f00474168b382180/image3.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1755\&quot; height=\&quot;648\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;After the attack payload, Pingora and the backend server are now âdesynced.â The backend server will wait until it thinks the rest of the partial /attack request header that Pingora forwarded is complete. When Pingora forwards a different userâs request, the two headers are combined from the backend serverâs perspective, and the attacker has now poisoned the other userâs response.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weâve since &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/824bdeefc61e121cc8861de1b35e8e8f39026ecd\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;patched&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; Pingora to switch the interpretation of subsequent bytes only once the upstream responds with &lt;code&gt;101 Switching Protocols&lt;/code&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We verified Cloudflare was &lt;b&gt;not affected&lt;/b&gt; for two reasons:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;The ingress CDN proxies do not have this improper behavior.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;The clients to our internal Pingora services do not attempt to &lt;a href=\&quot;https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HTTP_pipelining\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;pipeline&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; HTTP/1 requests. Furthermore, the Pingora service these clients talk directly with disables keep-alive on these &lt;code&gt;Upgrade&lt;/code&gt; requests by injecting a &lt;code&gt;Connection: close&lt;/code&gt; header; this prevents additional requests that would be sent â and subsequently smuggled â over the same connection.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h3 id=\&quot;2-http-1-0-close-delimiting-and-transfer-encoding\&quot;&gt;2. HTTP/1.0, close-delimiting, and transfer-encoding&lt;/h3&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#2-http-1-0-close-delimiting-and-transfer-encoding\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The reporter also demonstrated what &lt;i&gt;appeared&lt;/i&gt; to be a more classic âCL.TEâ desync-type attack, where the Pingora proxy would use Content-Length as framing while the backend would use Transfer-Encoding as framing:&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-Rust\&quot;&gt;GET / HTTP/1.0\nHost: example.com\nConnection: keep-alive\nTransfer-Encoding: identity, chunked\nContent-Length: 29\n\n0\n\nGET /admin HTTP/1.1\nX:\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;In the reporterâs example, Pingora would treat all subsequent bytes after the first GET / request header as part of that requestâs body, but the node.js backend server would interpret the body as chunked and ending at the zero-length chunk. There are actually a few things going on here:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ol&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pingoraâs chunked encoding recognition was quite barebones (only checking for whether &lt;code&gt;Transfer-Encoding&lt;/code&gt; was â&lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/9ac75d0356f449d26097e08bf49af14de6271727/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/common.rs#L146\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;chunked&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;â) and assumed that there could only be one encoding or &lt;code&gt;Transfer-Encoding&lt;/code&gt; header. But the RFC only &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-2.4.1\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;mandates&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; that the &lt;i&gt;final&lt;/i&gt; encoding must be &lt;code&gt;chunked&lt;/code&gt; to apply chunked framing. So per RFC, this request should have a chunked message body (if it were not HTTP/1.0 â more on that below).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pingora was &lt;i&gt;also &lt;/i&gt;not actually using the &lt;code&gt;Content-Length&lt;/code&gt; (because the Transfer-Encoding overrode the Content-Length &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-2.3\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;per RFC&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;). Because of the unrecognized Transfer-Encoding and the HTTP/1.0 version, the request body was &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-core/src/protocols/http/v1/server.rs#L817\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;instead treated as close-delimited&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (which means that the response bodyâs end is marked by closure of the underlying transport connection). An absence of framing headers would also trigger the same misinterpretation on HTTP/1.0. Although response bodies are allowed to be close-delimited, request bodies are &lt;i&gt;never&lt;/i&gt; close-delimited. In fact, this clarification is now explicitly called out as a separate note in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-4.1\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;RFC 9112&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is an HTTP/1.0 request that &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#appendix-C.2.3-1\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;did not define&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; Transfer-Encoding. The RFC &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.1-16\&quot;&gt;mandates&lt;/a&gt; that HTTP/1.0 requests containing Transfer-Encoding must âtreat the message as if the framing is faultyâ and close the connection. Parsers such as the ones in nginx and hyper just reject these requests to avoid ambiguous framing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ol&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/1jLbMNafmF96toxAPxj2Cm/8561b96a56dc0fc654476e33d0f34888/image2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1755\&quot; height=\&quot;1082\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;sup&gt;&lt;i&gt;When an attacker pipelines a partial HTTP request header after the HTTP/1.0 + Transfer-Encoding request, Pingora would incorrectly interpret that partial header as part of the same request, rather than as a distinct request. This enables the same kind of desync attack as described in the premature Upgrade example.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/sup&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This spoke to a more fundamental misreading of the RFC particularly in terms of response vs. request message framing. Weâve since fixed the improper &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/7f7166d62fa916b9f11b2eb8f9e3c4999e8b9023\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;multiple Transfer-Encoding parsing&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, adhere strictly to the request length guidelines such that HTTP request bodies can &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/40c3c1e9a43a86b38adeab8da7a2f6eba68b83ad\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;never be considered close-delimited&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and reject &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/fc904c0d2c679be522de84729ec73f0bd344963d\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;invalid Content-Length&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/87e2e2fb37edf9be33e3b1d04726293ae6bf2052\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;HTTP/1.0 + Transfer-Encoding&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; request messages. Further protections weâve added include &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/d3d2cf5ef4eca1e5d327fe282ec4b4ee474350c6\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;rejecting&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9110#name-connect\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CONNECT&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; requests by default because the HTTP proxy logic doesnât currently treat CONNECT as special for the purposes of CONNECT upgrade proxying, and these requests have special &lt;a href=\&quot;https://datatracker.ietf.org/doc/html/rfc9112#section-6.3-2.2\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;message framing rules&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. (Note that incoming CONNECT requests are &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/fundamentals/concepts/traffic-flow-cloudflare/#cloudflares-network\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;rejected&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; by the Cloudflare CDN.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When we investigated and instrumented our services internally, we found no requests arriving at our Pingora services that would have been misinterpreted. We found that downstream proxy layers in the CDN would forward as HTTP/1.1 only, reject ambiguous framing such as invalid Content-Length, and only forward a single &lt;code&gt;Transfer-Encoding: chunked&lt;/code&gt; header for chunked requests.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h3 id=\&quot;3-cache-key-construction\&quot;&gt;3. Cache key construction&lt;/h3&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#3-cache-key-construction\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;The researcher also reported one other cache poisoning vulnerability regarding default &lt;code&gt;CacheKey&lt;/code&gt; construction. The &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/blob/ef017ceb01962063addbacdab2a4fd2700039db5/pingora-cache/src/key.rs#L218\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;naive default implementation&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; factored in only the URI path (without other factors such as host header or upstream server HTTP scheme), which meant different hosts using the same HTTP path could collide and poison each otherâs cache.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This would affect users of the alpha proxy caching feature who chose to use the default &lt;code&gt;CacheKey&lt;/code&gt; implementation. We have since &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/commit/257b59ada28ed6cac039f67d0b71f414efa0ab6e\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;removed that default&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, because while using something like HTTP scheme + host + URI makes sense for many applications, we want users to be careful when constructing their cache keys for themselves. If their proxy logic will conditionally adjust the URI or method on the upstream request, for example, that logic likely also must be factored into the cache key scheme to avoid poisoning.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Internally, Cloudflareâs &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/cache/how-to/cache-keys/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;default cache key&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; uses a number of factors to prevent cache key poisoning, and never made use of the previously provided default.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;recommendation\&quot;&gt;Recommendation&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#recommendation\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;If you use Pingora as a proxy, upgrade to &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/cloudflare/pingora/releases/tag/0.8.0\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Pingora 0.8.0&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; at your earliest convenience.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We apologize for the impact this vulnerability may have had on Pingora users. As Pingora earns its place as critical Internet infrastructure beyond Cloudflare, we believe itâs important for the framework to promote use of strict RFC compliance by default and will continue this effort. Very few users of the framework should have to deal with the same âwild Internetâ that Cloudflare does. Our intention is that stricter adherence to the latest RFC standards by default will harden security for Pingora users and move the Internet as a whole toward best practices.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;disclosure-and-response-timeline\&quot;&gt;Disclosure and response timeline&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#disclosure-and-response-timeline\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;- 2025â12â02: Upgradeâbased smuggling reported via bug bounty.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026â01â13: TransferâEncoding / HTTP/1.0 parsing issues reported.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026-01-18: Default cache key construction issue reported.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026â01â29 to 2026â02â13: Fixes validated with the reporter. Work on more RFC-compliance checks continues.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026-02-25: Cache key default removal and additional RFC checks validated with researcher.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026â03-02: Pingora 0.8.0 released.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;- 2026-03-04: CVE advisories published.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;acknowledgements\&quot;&gt;Acknowledgements&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#acknowledgements\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;We thank Rajat Raghav (xclow3n) for the report, detailed reproductions, and verification of the fixes through our bug bounty program. &quot;],&quot;imgPreview&quot;:[0,&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/O1sZXQ8Arqkc2KpknClSE/aa66dd33b86bd91a31a346ce454bbb09/Fixing_request_smuggling_vulnerabilities_in_Pingora_OSS_deployments-OG.png&quot;]}],&quot;publicly_index&quot;:[0,true]}],&quot;initialReadingTime&quot;:[0,&quot;7&quot;],&quot;relatedPosts&quot;:[1,[[0,{&quot;id&quot;:[0,&quot;7yIVIjWT0unNpdtbhOCVnh&quot;],&quot;title&quot;:[0,&quot;Active defense: introducing a stateful vulnerability scanner for APIs&quot;],&quot;slug&quot;:[0,&quot;vulnerability-scanner&quot;],&quot;excerpt&quot;:[0,&quot;Cloudflareâs new Web and API Vulnerability Scanner helps teams proactively find logic flaws. By using AI to build API call graphs, we identify vulnerabilities that standard defensive tools miss.&quot;],&quot;featured&quot;:[0,false],&quot;html&quot;:[0,&quot;&lt;p&gt;Security is traditionally a game of defense. You build walls, set up gates, and write rules to block traffic that looks suspicious. For years, Cloudflare has been a leader in this space: our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/application-services/products/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Application Security platform&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; is designed to catch attacks in flight, dropping malicious requests at the edge before they ever reach your origin. But for &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/security/api/what-is-api-security/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API security&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, defensive posturing isnât enough.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thatâs why today, we are launching the beta of Cloudflareâs Web and API Vulnerability Scanner.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We are starting with the most pervasive and difficult-to-catch threat on the OWASP API Top 10: &lt;a href=\&quot;https://owasp.org/API-Security/editions/2023/en/0xa1-broken-object-level-authorization/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Broken Object Level Authorization&lt;/u&gt;, or BOLA.&lt;/a&gt; We will add more vulnerability scan types over time, including both API and web application threats.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The most dangerous API vulnerabilities today arenât generic injection attacks or malformed requests that a WAF can easily spot. They are logic flawsâperfectly valid HTTP requests that meet the protocol and application spec but defy the business logic.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To find these, you canât just wait for an attack. You have to actively hunt for them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Web and API Vulnerability Scanner will be available first for &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api-shield/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API Shield&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; customers. Read on to learn why we are focused on API security scans for this first release.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;why-purely-defensive-security-misses-the-mark\&quot;&gt;Why purely defensive security misses the mark&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#why-purely-defensive-security-misses-the-mark\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;In the web application world, vulnerabilities often look like syntax errors. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/security/threats/sql-injection/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;SQL injection&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; attempt looks like code where data should be. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/security/threats/cross-site-scripting/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;cross-site scripting (XSS)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; attack looks like a script tag in a form field. These have signatures.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;API vulnerabilities are different. To illustrate, letâs imagine a food delivery mobile app that communicates solely with an API on the backend. Letâs take the orders endpoint:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Endpoint Definition: &lt;/b&gt;&lt;code&gt;&lt;b&gt;/api/v1/orders&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;table&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Method&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Resource Path&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Description&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;GET&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;/api/v1/orders/{order_id}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Check Status.&lt;/b&gt; Returns the tracking status of a specific order (e.g., &amp;quot;Kitchen is preparing&amp;quot;).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;PATCH&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;/api/v1/orders/{order_id}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;b&gt;Update Order.&lt;/b&gt; Allows the user to modify the drop-off location or add delivery instructions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;p&gt;In a broken authorization attack like BOLA, User A (the attacker) requests to update the delivery address of a paid-for order belonging to User B (the victim). The attacker simply inserts User Bâs &lt;code&gt;{order_id}&lt;/code&gt; in the &lt;code&gt;PATCH&lt;/code&gt; request.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here is what that request looks like, with â8821â as User Bâs order ID. Notice that User A is fully authenticated with their own valid token:&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-javascript\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-javascript\&quot;&gt;PATCH /api/v1/orders/8821 HTTP/1.1\nHost: api.example.com\nAuthorization: Bearer &amp;lt;User_A_Valid_Token&amp;gt;\nContent-Type: application/json\n\n{\n  &amp;quot;delivery_address&amp;quot;: &amp;quot;123 Attacker Way, Apt 4&amp;quot;,\n  &amp;quot;instructions&amp;quot;: &amp;quot;Leave at front door, ring bell&amp;quot;\n}\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;The request headers are valid. The authentication token is valid. The schema is correct. To a standard WAF, this request looks perfect. A bot management offering may even be fooled if a human is manually sending the attack requests.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;User A will now get Bâs food delivered to them! The vulnerability exists because the API endpoint fails to validate if User A actually has permission to view or update user Bâs data. This is a failure of logic, not syntax. To fix this, the API developer could implement a simple check: &lt;code&gt;if (order.userID != user.ID) throw Unauthorized;&lt;/code&gt;&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/ZOOAjAYcZqzDYg9snYASL/65940a740ba7ef294b719e76d37f3cdd/BLOG-3161_2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;BLOG-3161 2\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1728\&quot; height=\&quot;1428\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;You can detect these types of vulnerabilities by actively sending API test traffic or passively listening to existing API traffic. Finding these vulnerabilities through passive scanning requires context. Last year &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/changelog/2025-11-12-bola-attack-detection/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;we launched BOLA vulnerability detection&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; for API Shield. This detection automatically finds these vulnerabilities by passively scanning customer traffic for usage anomalies. To be successful with this type of scanning, you need to know what a &amp;quot;valid&amp;quot; API call looks like, what the variable parameters are, how a typical user behaves, and how the API behaves when those parameters are manipulated.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet there are reasons security teams may not have any of that context, even with access to API Shieldâs BOLA vulnerability detection. Development environments may need to be tested but lack user traffic. Production environments may (thankfully) have a lack of attack traffic yet still need analysis, and so on. In these circumstances, and to be proactive in general, teams can turn to Dynamic Application Security Testing (DAST). By creating net-new traffic profiles intended specifically for security testing, DAST tools can look for vulnerabilities in any environment at any time.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Unfortunately, traditional DAST tools have a high barrier to entry. They are often difficult to configure, require you to manually upload and maintain Swagger/OpenAPI files, struggle to authenticate correctly against modern complex login flows, and can simply lack any API-specific security tests (e.g. BOLA).&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;cloudflares-api-scanning-advantage\&quot;&gt;Cloudflareâs API scanning advantage&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#cloudflares-api-scanning-advantage\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;In the food delivery order example above, we assumed the attacker could find a valid order to modify. While there are often avenues for attackers to gather this type of intelligence in a live production environment, in a security testing exercise you must create your own objects before testing the APIâs authorization controls. For typical DAST scans, this can be a problem, because many scanners treat each individual request on its own. This method fails to chain requests together in the logical pattern necessary to find broken authorization vulnerabilities. Legacy DAST scanners can also exist as an island within your security tooling and orchestration environment, preventing their findings from being shared or viewed in context.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Vulnerability scanning from Cloudflare is different for a few key reasons.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;First, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/security-center/security-insights/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Security Insights&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; will list results from our new scans alongside any existing Cloudflare security findings for added context. Youâll see all your posture management information in one place.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Second,&lt;b&gt; &lt;/b&gt;we already know your APIâs inputs and outputs. If you are an API Shield customer, Cloudflare already understands your API. Our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api-shield/security/api-discovery/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API Discovery&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api-shield/management-and-monitoring/endpoint-management/schema-learning/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Schema Learning&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; features passively catalog your endpoints and learn your traffic patterns. While youâll need to manually upload an OpenAPI spec to get started for our initial release, you will be able to get started quickly without one in a future release.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Third, because we sit at the edge, we can turn passive traffic inspection knowledge into active intelligence. It will be easy to verify BOLA vulnerability detection risks (found via traffic inspection) by sending net-new HTTP requests with the vulnerability scanner.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And finally, we have built a new, stateful DAST platform, as we detail below. Most scanners require hours of setup to &amp;quot;teach&amp;quot; the tool how to talk to your API. With Cloudflare, you can effectively skip that step and get started quickly. You provide the API credentials, and weâll use your API schemas to automatically construct a scan plan.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;building-automatic-scan-plans\&quot;&gt;Building automatic scan plans&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#building-automatic-scan-plans\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;APIs are commonly documented using &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.openapis.org/what-is-openapi\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;OpenAPI schemas&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. These schemas denote the host, method, and path (commonly, an âendpointâ) along with the expected parameters of incoming requests and resulting responses. In order to automatically build a scan plan, we must first make sense of these API specifications for any given API to be scanned.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our scanner works by building up an API call graph from an OpenAPI document and subsequently walking it, using attacker and owner contexts. Owners create resources, attackers subsequently try to access them. Attackers are fully authenticated with their own set of valid credentials. If an attacker successfully reads, modifies or deletes an unowned resource, an authorization vulnerability is found.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Consider for example the above delivery order with ID 8821. For the server-side resource to exist, it needed to be originally created by an owner, most likely in a âgenesisâ &lt;code&gt;POST&lt;/code&gt; request with no or minimal dependencies (previous necessary calls and resulting data). Modelling the API as a call graph, such an endpoint constitutes a node with no or few incoming edges (dependencies). Any subsequent request, such as the attackerâs &lt;code&gt;PATCH&lt;/code&gt; above, then has a &lt;i&gt;data dependency&lt;/i&gt; (the data is &lt;code&gt;order_id&lt;/code&gt;) on the genesis request (the &lt;code&gt;POST&lt;/code&gt;). Without all data provided, the &lt;code&gt;PATCH&lt;/code&gt; cannot proceed.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/7q0y7XZJE411gzhRuo9UjG/f7722c58e6cac751a1db44b612098a7b/BLOG-3161_3.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;BLOG-3161 3\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1176\&quot; height=\&quot;1642\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here we see in purple arrows the nodes in this API graph that are necessary to visit to add a note to an order via the POST &lt;code&gt;/api/v1/orders/{order_id}/note/{note_id}&lt;/code&gt; endpoint. &lt;b&gt;Importantly, none of the steps or logic shown in the diagram is available in the OpenAPI specification!&lt;/b&gt; It must be inferred logically through some other means, and that is exactly what our vulnerability scanner will do automatically.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In order to reliably and automatically plan scans across a variety of APIs, we must accurately model these endpoint relationships from scratch. However, two problems arise: data quality of API specifications is not guaranteed, and even functionally complete schemas can have ambiguous naming schemes. Consider a simplified OpenAPI specification for the above API, which might look like&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-javascript\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-javascript\&quot;&gt;openapi: 3.0.0\ninfo:\n  title: Order API\n  version: 1.0.0\npaths:\n  /api/v1/orders:\n    post:\n      summary: Create an order\n      requestBody:\n        required: true\n        content:\n          application/json:\n            schema:\n              type: object\n              properties:\n                product:\n                  type: string\n                count:\n                  type: integer\n              required:\n                - product\n                - count\n      responses:\n        &amp;#039;201&amp;#039;:\n          description: Item created successfully\n          content:\n            application/json:\n              schema:\n                type: object\n                properties:\n                  result:\n                    type: object\n                    properties:\n                      id:\n                        type: integer\n                      created_at:\n                        type: integer\n                  errors:\n                    type: array\n                    items:\n                      type: string\n  /api/v1/orders/{order_id}:\n    patch:\n      summary: Modify an order by ID\n      parameters:\n        - name: order_id\n          in: path\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;We can see that the &lt;code&gt;POST&lt;/code&gt; endpoint returns responses such as&lt;/p&gt;\n            &lt;pre class=\&quot;language-json\&quot;&gt;&lt;code class=\&quot;language-json\&quot;&gt;{\n    &amp;quot;result&amp;quot;: {\n        &amp;quot;id&amp;quot;: 8821,\n        &amp;quot;created_at&amp;quot;: 1741476777\n    },\n   &amp;quot;errors&amp;quot;: []\n}\n&lt;/pre&gt;&lt;/code&gt;\n            &lt;p&gt;To a human observer, it is quickly evident that &lt;code&gt;$.result.id&lt;/code&gt; is the value to be injected in &lt;code&gt;order_id&lt;/code&gt; for the &lt;code&gt;PATCH&lt;/code&gt; endpoint. The &lt;code&gt;id&lt;/code&gt; property might also be called &lt;code&gt;orderId, value&lt;/code&gt; or something else, and be nested arbitrarily. These subtle inconsistencies in OpenAPI documents of arbitrary shape are intractable for heuristics-based approaches.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our scanner uses Cloudflareâs own &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/workers-ai/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Workers AI&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; platform to tackle this fuzzy problem space. Models such as &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/workers-ai/models/gpt-oss-120b/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;OpenAIâs open-weight gpt-oss-120b&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; are powerful enough to match data dependencies reliably, and to generate realistic fake&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;data where necessary, essentially filling in the blanks of OpenAPI specifications. Leveraging &lt;a href=\&quot;https://platform.openai.com/docs/guides/structured-outputs\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;structured outputs&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, the model produces a representation of the API call graph for our scanner to walk, injecting attacker and owner credentials appropriately.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This approach tackles the problem of needing human intelligence to infer authorization and data relationships in OpenAPI schemas with artificial intelligence to do the same. Structured outputs bridge the gap from the natural language world of gpt-oss back to machine-executable instructions. In addition to Workers AI solving the planning problem, self-hosting on Workers AI means our system automatically benefits from Cloudflareâs highly available, globally distributed architecture.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h3 id=\&quot;built-on-proven-foundations\&quot;&gt;Built on proven foundations&lt;/h3&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#built-on-proven-foundations\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;Building a vulnerability scanner that customers will trust with their API credentials demands proven infrastructure. We did not reinvent the wheel here. Instead, we integrated services that have been validated and deployed across Cloudflare for two crucial components of our scanner platform: the scannerâs control plane and the scannerâs secrets store.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The scanner&amp;#39;s control plane integrates with &lt;a href=\&quot;https://github.com/temporalio/temporal\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Temporal&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; for Scan Orchestration, on which other internal services at Cloudflare already rely. The complexity of the numerous test plans executed in each Scan is effectively managed by Temporal&amp;#39;s durable execution framework.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The entire backend is written in Rust, which is widely adopted at Cloudflare for infrastructure services. This lets us reuse internal libraries and share architectural patterns across teams. It also positions our scanner for potential future integration with other Cloudflare systems like FL2 or our test framework &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/20-percent-internet-upgrade/#step-2-testing-and-automated-rollouts\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Flamingo&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; â enabling scenarios where scanning could coordinate more tightly with edge request handling or testing infrastructure.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h4 id=\&quot;credential-security-through-hashicorps-vault-transit-secret-engine\&quot;&gt;Credential security through HashiCorpâs Vault Transit Secret Engine&lt;/h4&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#credential-security-through-hashicorps-vault-transit-secret-engine\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;Scanning for broken authentication and broken authorization vulnerabilities requires handling API user credentials. Cloudflare takes this responsibility very seriously.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We ensure that our public API layer has minimal access to unencrypted customer credentials by using HashiCorp&amp;#39;s &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developer.hashicorp.com/vault/docs/secrets/transit\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Vault Transit Secret Engine&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (TSE) for encryption-as-a-service. Immediately upon submission, credentials are encrypted by TSEâwhich handles the encryption but does not store the ciphertextâand are subsequently stored on Cloudflare infrastructure.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our API is not authorized to decrypt this data. Instead, decryption occurs only at the last stage when a TestPlan makes a request to the customer&amp;#39;s infrastructure. Only the Worker executing the test is authorized to request decryption, a restriction we strengthen using strict typing with additional safety rails inside Rust to enforce minimal access to decryption methods.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We further secure our customersâ credentials through regular rotation and periodic rewraps using TSE to mitigate risk. This process means we only interact with the new ciphertext, and the original secret is kept unviewable.&lt;/p&gt;\n    &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n      &lt;h2 id=\&quot;whats-next\&quot;&gt;Whatâs next?&lt;/h2&gt;\n      &lt;a href=\&quot;#whats-next\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-start-5\&quot;&gt;\n        &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n      &lt;/a&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;p&gt;We are releasing BOLA vulnerability scanning starting today as an Open Beta for all API Shield customers, and are working on future API threat scans for future release. Via the Cloudflare API, you can trigger scans, manage configuration, and retrieve results programmatically to integrate directly into your &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/serverless/glossary/what-is-ci-cd/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;CI/CD pipelines&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; or security dashboards. For API Shield Customers: check the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api-shield/security/vulnerability-scanner/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;developer docs&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to start scanning your endpoints for BOLA vulnerabilities today.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We are starting with BOLA vulnerabilities because they are the hardest API vulnerability to solve and the highest risk for our customers. However, this scanning engine is built to be extensible.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the near future, we plan to expand the scannerâs capabilities to cover the most popular of the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://owasp.org/www-project-top-ten/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;OWASP &lt;/u&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;u&gt;Web&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;u&gt; Top 10&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; as well: classic web vulnerabilities like SQL injection (SQLi) and cross-site scripting (XSS). To be notified upon release, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/lp/security-week/vulnerability-scanner/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;sign up for the waitlist here&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and youâll be first to learn when we expand the engine to general web application
